Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 900-1520 CE ♥

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ quasi-polity ♥ The Zapotec state fragmented into numerous competing polities after the end of the IIIA period, each politically independent of the others.[1][2]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ none; personal union ♥ Marriage alliances between the elite of the Zapotec and Mixtec societies during this period were recorded by the relaciones (16th century Spanish writers). Alliances were created for many possible reasons, including political gain, status and increased access to farmland and resources.[3] No permanent unions between polities; these alliances seem between individuals.

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Monte Alban IIIB and IV ♥
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥ The process of political balkanisation which started at the end of the MA IIIA period continued into this period until the Spanish invasion. Separate kingdoms formed, with Monte Alban still occupied but with a much reduced population.[4]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Spanish colonial rule ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ ♥

♠ Capital ♣ none ♥ There was no single capital in the valley. Instead there were a series of smaller polities, each with a head town and other smaller settlements.[5]


♠ Language ♣ Zapotec ♥ [6]
♠ Linguistic Family ♣ Otomanguean ♥ Both the Zapotecan (Zapotec and Chatino) and Mixtecan (Mixtec, Cuicatec and Trique) languages belong to the Otomanguean language family, which also includes: Otopamean, Chinantecan Popolocan, Huave, Amuzgo and Manguean language groups.[7]
♠ Language Genus ♣ ♥
♠ Religious Tradition ♣ ♥

Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

The Monte Alban V period is generally known for the balkianisation of polities in the valley.[8] The length assigned to the Monte Albán V phase is due to the consistency of ceramic style throughout the period, and lack of evidence for major changes in other archaeological remains. There may have been many changes over these centuries, but it has been argued that the conservatism of ceramics suggests a time of relative political stability.[9] The primary evidence for this period comes from the accounts written by the Spanish after they arrived in the 1520s. This means that there is much more information than previous periods for certain aspects of life for the inhabitants of Oaxaca, such as the name for the head of the ruling class (the coquitao or “great lord”) and full-time priests (bigaña),[10] but less information in other respects as there were no longer any monumental buildings or large settlements being constructed. The unified valley under the Zapotec state had gone by this period, replaced by around 15-20 smaller polities[11][12] which were often fighting one another (as suggested by the widespread use of fortifications and descriptions in the ethnohistoric records),[13] but the overall population of the valley continued to increase to the highest number yet reached in the valley.[14] In addition, craft work and specialisation became more widespread, with more finely decorated ceramics being produced throughout the valley and were not just concentrated in the main settlements as before.[15]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ ♥ As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[16]

"Table 10.1. Population of Late Postclassic polities in the Central Valleys of Oaxaca" + "Table 10.2. Population of other Late Postclassic polities in highland Oaxaca." = ~ 61 polities.[17] Polity territory = total area / 61?

♠ Polity Population ♣ [8,127-10,836] ♥ The overall population of the valley increased during this period, but was divided into numerous (15-20) smaller political entities.[18] The population of the whole valley (based on the total of settlement population estimates) would have been 95,523-229,581 people.[19] A very coarse estimate of the average polity population is taken as the average between the higher and lower population estimates for the whole valley (162,552 people) divided by 15 and 20 to give a higher and lower range of polity size (10,836 and 8,127 people respectively). The precise numbers for the polity population estimates should not be taken as accurate predictions of polity population size.

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [2-3] ♥ levels. For each of the 15-20 smaller polities, there was a head town with supporting villages and hamlets.[29] By 900 CE, Monte Alban was no longer a primary center, although it continued to be occupied.[30]

1. Main town of the polity

2. Village
3. Hamlet

"Table 10.9. Monte Alban V population hierarchy in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[31]

Valley of Oaxaca: Level I: 11504-13831; II: 6324-6649; III: 1192-3430; IV: 736-953; V: 486-665; VI: 308-545; VII: 204-297; No rank: 8-199.[32]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [2-3] ♥ levels. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[33] The levels of organisation are supported by the archaeological evidence which suggests that there was a head town in each of the polities, with supporting villages and hamlets.[34]

♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥ levels. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the presence of full-time priests, or bigaña, during this period. [35] The bigaña were ranked beneath the uija-táo (or great seer) and above the ueza-eche, huetete colanij (sacrifice or diviner),[36] although the extent to which these ranks can be inferred back to the whole period are not known.

1. First rank-uija-táo-“great seer”

2. Second rank-vuijatáo copa pitáo bigaña-“priest”/ bigaña-“young priest” or “student priest”
3. Third rank-ueza-eche, huetete colanij-“sacrifice” or “diviner”

♠ Military levels ♣ 2 ♥ levels. Spanish written records describe the presence of military officers and soldiers (civilian conscripts) during this period.[37]

1. Military officers

2. Individual soldier

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥ Descriptions in the Spanish relaciones (Spanish written documents from the time of the Spanish conquest) provide evidence for the presence of military officers in charge of competing armies at the end of this period. Their presence in the centuries before the Spanish conquest is inferred.[38][39]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ Inferred present from the descriptions of military officers and armies in the Spanish relaciones.[40][41]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ Spanish written records describe the presence of full-time priests (bigaña) who lived in temples and were the sons of the Zapotec nobility. A profesional priesthood is therefore inferred to be present, although the extent to which this information can be extended back to the entire period is not known.[42][43]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ inferred absent ♥ Spanish written records refer to bureaucratic positions, such as the positions of tribute collector, ward boss and golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who collected goods and services from surrounding villages.[44][45] However, the evidence we have does not shed any light on whether these were full-time specialists.[46] Charles Spencer commented: 'In the Zapotec case, we have a few 16th-century sources, but they are far from ideal in terms of providing details about Zapotec governance in the 16th century, let alone the time of the early Monte Alban state (ca. 300 B.C. through A.D. 800). Flannery and Marcus have done a fine job of pulling together what can be gleaned from the 16th century sources (e.g., Flannery 1983 "The Legacy of the Early Urban Period: An Ethnohistoric Approach" in The Cloud People). Taking a broad view of administration [...] one can identify several governing specialists in the 16th sources, for example: coquitao (king), coquihualao (prince), xoana (minor noble), golaba (lord's solicitor or barrio head), uija-tao (high priest), bigaña (lower-ranking priests). There may have been even more specialists in the 16th century, but [...] the ethnohistoric sources don't provide adequate documentation'.[47]

♠ Source of support ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[48]

♠ Examination system ♣ ♥ Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[49]

♠ Merit promotion ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[50]

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ unknown. Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[51] Our understanding of precolonial Zapotec administrative structures (both in terms of official positions and built architecture) is limited, and Gary Feinman commented that 'My own view is that 16th century governance was rather different from that at Monte Albán in that the 16th century governance was centered more expressly on palaces, while earlier Monte Albán phase governance was less so'.[52] If this was the case for this period, administrative buildings may not have existed independently of elite residences.

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ absent ♥ Only limited evidence for roads has been found (in earlier phases at Monte Alban), and these appear to have been restricted to within settlements. We asked Gary Feinman about roads in Oaxacan polities and he said: "It depends on what you mean by roads. There are definite roads/accessways within sites. Blanton defines some at Monte Albán and Linda [Nicholas] and I defined some at El Palmillo. These likely were not paved, but they may have been banked and were cleared. Between sites there are known 16th century trails, which were likely used for a long, long time. Again, they likely were not paved, but there were no beasts of burden."[62] Coded absent: we do not count accessways within settlements or paths and trails not constructed deliberately as roads.
♠ Bridges ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for bridges in prehispanic Valley of Oaxaca.[63]
♠ Canals ♣ inferred absent ♥ Although canals were present, they would not have been large enough to use as transport.[64]
♠ Ports ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.

Information[edit]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ The Zapotec unit of yaguen (26-27cm, or the length of a forearm) was used as a measure, and many stone monuments at Monte Alban are multiples of a yaguen.[72]
♠ Area ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[73]
♠ Volume ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[74]
♠ Weight ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[75]
♠ Time ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[76]
♠ Geometrical ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[77]
♠ Other ♣ ♥ unknown. Evidence for consistent measurements other than the yaguen length has not been found.[78]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation was written in Spanish after the end of this period.[79]
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Written records ♣ inferred present ♥ Zapotec writing and counting systems were recorded by the Spanish after the invasion.[80] Mesoamerican civilizations, including the Maya, Aztec, Mixtec and Zapotec all possessed "a true form of writing: a series of hieroglyphs arranged in vertical columns and in many instances combined with numerals. The glyphs were at least indirectly related to a spoken language." Zapotec and Mixtec belong to the Otomanguean language family while the Aztec and and Maya belong to the Utoaztecan and Macro-Mayan, respectively. Zapotec writing system is considered the oldest (from c600 BCE). Zapotec inscriptions are considered true writing, since the inscriptions had verbs.[81]
♠ Script ♣ inferred present ♥ Zapotec writing and counting systems were recorded by the Spanish after the invasion.[82] Detailed documentation was written in Spanish after the end of this period.[83]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation was written in Spanish after the end of this period.[84]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation was written in Spanish after the end of this period.[85]

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ present ♥ Genealogical registers were recorded in the previous periods (IIIB-IV),[86] and lists assumed to have continued in existence during this period.
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ Spanish written records refer to the Zapotec calendars, one 365 day calendar and one 260 day ‘ritual’ calendar called ‘pije/ piye’, which links to the Zapotec word ‘pé’ for life).[87]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[88]
♠ Religious literature ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[89]
♠ Practical literature ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[90]
♠ History ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[91]
♠ Philosophy ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[92]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[93]
♠ Fiction ♣ absent ♥ Detailed documentation of life in the Valley of Oaxaca were written only after the Spanish conquest in the 1520s.[94]

♠ Plate armor ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[157][158]

Naval technology[edit]

♠ Small vessels (canoes, etc) ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.
♠ Merchant ships pressed into service ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.
♠ Specialized military vessels ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.

Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ Many settlements continued to be located on hilltops during this period.[159]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ unknown ♥ Monte Albán was built with a 3km defensive wall along the shallower slopes of the hill, and wooden palisades may have been present.[160]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ present ♥ The defensive wall around Monte Alban was made of earth and stone.[161][162]
♠ Ditch ♣ absent ♥ The presence of a ditch has not been recorded.[163]
♠ Moat ♣ absent ♥ There is no evidence for a moat at Monte Alban or other settlements.[164]
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ present ♥ Many settlements show evidence for fortifications from this period, for example: “…the rocky summit of the Yagul hill [Tlacolula region] was fortified during Period V with the same kind of dry-laid stone masonry walls used at the Mitla Fortress.”[165]
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ unknown ♥
♠ Fortified camps ♣ absent ♥ Evidence for fortified camps has not been found for this period.[166]
♠ Complex fortifications ♣ inferred present ♥ The fortifications along the northern sector of Monte Alban consisted of an inner and outer wall, although the outer wall may have been much older (constructed in the Late I or II phases).[167]
♠ Long walls ♣ ♥ Evidence for long walls has not been found in the valley.[168]
♠ Modern fortifications ♣ absent ♥ Modern fortifications were not present in prehispanic times.[169]

Archaeological proxies:

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ inferred present ♥ Data for Yucatan peninsula: "In Mayan populations we similar pattern of trauma but with an over all higher prevalence in the population. Between the pre-classical period (starting in 1000 BCE) and the post-classical period (ending in 1519 CE) trauma prevalence in the Yucatan peninsula reflects the political and social climate at the time (Tieslar and Cucina 2012). In the pre-classical period trauma levels are low ... trauma is visible in 5.4% of males, 18.75% in females and a total of 13.3% of the population (Tieslar and Cucina 2012). In the post-contact, post-classical period during a period of colonial warfare waged by the Spanish trauma is visible in 25% of males, 12% of females and a total of 25% of the total population (Tieslar and Cucina 2012)."[170]

♠ Settlement destruction ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ RP responsible for original coding; EC found more recent and relevant sources and used them to code this section anew.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Ritual System

Information is coded using archaeological variables because of lack of information about discreet rituals.

Rituals coded below are inferred from previous Monte Alban polities. There is not much information from this polity because there was certainly a decline in Monte Alban. The Grand Plaza fell into ruin, some temples remained in use although ruined. [172] It is possible there was substantial variation from traditional practice because foreign influence is seen in pottery and metal work in this polity.[173]


Burials-seven tombs found at Monte Alban, architectural style of Monte Alban-cross-chambers down stairs. (Whitecotton p.97), more tombs found at Zaachila. [174] Grave goods-Offerings of artifacts of a different style to previous time-polychrome pottery, bone carvings and lots of gold and metal work, including largest known silver vessel pre-columbian-of post-classic Mixtec tradition. At Zaachila more typical Mixteca-Puebla objects found and individuals given Mixtec codices. [175] Decoration-decoration from tombs at Zachila shows males grasping human femurs, more secular in nature than previous polity decorations. [176]

Gatherings Ritual Space Architecture-at Mitla, South Group of temple pyramids and Adobe Group also pyramidal (dwarved by palatial structures-less important) [177] Monte Alban Grand Plaza fell into ruin, some temples remained in use although ruined. [178]


Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ patron deity ritual ♥ NOTE: The following refers to the seventeenth century, but it seems like a fairly reasonable inference that the ritual system described by Spanish witnesses at that time emerged in the Post-Classic phase. "A major ceremony in honor of the patron deity of the city-state, usually Pezeelao, was held on a specific day assigned to him in the sacred calendar, perhaps the last day of the ritual cycle, 13 Lao or Pece Lao, his probable namesake. Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart. This ceremony involved a human sacrifice, usually a war captive, and was accompanied by feasting, drinking, music, and dancing, as were all major ceremonies. [...] David Tavarez (2005) has reported ritual songs in early eighteenth-century documents from the Sierra Juarez that relate to Zapotec deities. It seems likely that these ritual songs were sung at one or another of these ceremonies honoring each of these deities. In Zapotec, the songs were called dij dola nicachi, or 'wooden drum songs,' and were performed in a plaza in front of the entire community by musicians (belao in Zapotec) including singers who sang the songs to the beat of wooden drums (nicachi in Zapotec, teponaxtli in Nahuatl)." [179] "The ceremonies [in honour of the patron deity] involved the participation of all the communities within the city-state whose member brought offerings of quail, turkeys, dogs, feathers, and precious stones to the major temple in the capital. The high point of the celebration involved the sacrifice of a war captive who was placed over a sacrificial stone in the temple and had his heart removed and offered to the patron deity." [180]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart." [181] Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources.

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ consultation of colaní priests ♥ NOTE: The following refers to the seventeenth century, but it seems like a fairly reasonable inference that the ritual system described by Spanish witnesses at that time emerged in the Post-Classic phase. "Colaní were involved in the life of every Zapotec commoner from the day they were born until the day they died. Colaní gave Zapotecs their principal names based on the day in the sacred calendar on which the latter was born. The infants' vocations were likewise predicted by colaní. When children reached the age of reason, colaní introduced them to their tone, or animal companion, who accompanied them throughout life. When people planned to marry, colaní determined the compatibility or incompatibility of the couple and predicted if the child would be born alive, die, or be gravely ill. Following birth, the colaní performed the ritual of the sweat bath for the new mother and sacrificed a turkey to Pitao Huichaana in the new parents' home.
"Any serious illness or injury that a person suffered required consultation with a colaní, who would determine from the sacred calendar the deity responsible and perform the necessary rituals to appease that deity. [...] When a person died the family consulted colaní who offered sacrifices to Pitao Pezeelao, to keep further death and disease away from their doors, and to Pitao Huichaana, to purify the room in which the deceased had succumbed. [...] "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [219] It is this basic act of consultation--person approaches colaní, colaní consults sacred book and/or performs some form of divination, colaní provides response--that will be coded here as "most widespread" ritual.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [220] Not only that, but colaní were also consulted for all major life rituals: pregnancy, birth, naming the child, deciding the child's vocation, marriage, illness and death [221]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ monthly ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [222] Not only that, but colaní were also consulted for all major life rituals: pregnancy, birth, naming the child, deciding the child's vocation, marriage, illness and death [223]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. ♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ in absolute numbers. 2 is the minimum number of participants required: a petitioner, and a colaní. Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources [226].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ whole polity ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The sixty-one colaní recorded by Balslobre included five women[...]. Although colaní were predominantly males, the inclusion of women is not surprising in Zapotec culture where, since Prehispanic times, males and females were relatively equal in status (Lind and Urcid 2010:11). From the list of sixty-one colaní cited by Heinrich Berlin (1988:25-28), it is apparent that the children of colaní often became colaní themselves. [...] However, according to Berlin (1988:25), anyone acquiring a book, finding a colaní to take them as an apprentice, and willing to study could become a colaní." [227]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The colaní used their ritual books to serve the needs of commoners, although it is possible that nobles consulted them as well." [228]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The colaní used their ritual books to serve the needs of commoners, although it is possible that nobles consulted them as well." Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ consultation of colaní priests ♥ NOTE: The following refers to the seventeenth century, but it seems like a fairly reasonable inference that the ritual system described by Spanish witnesses at that time emerged in the Post-Classic phase. "Colaní were involved in the life of every Zapotec commoner from the day they were born until the day they died. Colaní gave Zapotecs their principal names based on the day in the sacred calendar on which the latter was born. The infants' vocations were likewise predicted by colaní. When children reached the age of reason, colaní introduced them to their tone, or animal companion, who accompanied them throughout life. When people planned to marry, colaní determined the compatibility or incompatibility of the couple and predicted if the child would be born alive, die, or be gravely ill. Following birth, the colaní performed the ritual of the sweat bath for the new mother and sacrificed a turkey to Pitao Huichaana in the new parents' home. "Any serious illness or injury that a person suffered required consultation with a colaní, who would determine from the sacred calendar the deity responsible and perform the necessary rituals to appease that deity. [...] When a person died the family consulted colaní who offered sacrifices to Pitao Pezeelao, to keep further death and disease away from their doors, and to Pitao Huichaana, to purify the room in which the deceased had succumbed. [...]"The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [261] It is this basic act of consultation--person approaches colaní, colaní consults sacred book and/or performs some form of divination, colaní provides response--that will be coded here as "most frequent" ritual.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [262] Not only that, but colaní were also consulted for all major life rituals: pregnancy, birth, naming the child, deciding the child's vocation, marriage, illness and death [263]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ monthly ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [264] Not only that, but colaní were also consulted for all major life rituals: pregnancy, birth, naming the child, deciding the child's vocation, marriage, illness and death [265]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The omens and dreams a person experienced were interpreted by colaní who consulted ritual books and determined the deity responsible, usually Pitao Peeze, the god of omens. If an object were lost or stolen, colaní were contacted to secure its recovery. Colaní were also consulted regarding propitious days for merchants to begin their trading expeditions, for hunters and fishermen to undertake their hunting or fishing expeditions, and even for persons who planned to construct a new house." [266] Not only that, but colaní were also consulted for all major life rituals: pregnancy, birth, naming the child, deciding the child's vocation, marriage, illness and death [267] Thomas Cressy: The quote for frequency seems to infer that everyone could participate in this, so I have coded this as 'not applicable' instead of 'monthly'.
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ in absolute numbers. 2 is the minimum number of participants required: a petitioner, and a colaní. Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources [268].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ whole polity ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The sixty-one colaní recorded by Balslobre included five women[...]. Although colaní were predominantly males, the inclusion of women is not surprising in Zapotec culture where, since Prehispanic times, males and females were relatively equal in status (Lind and Urcid 2010:11). From the list of sixty-one colaní cited by Heinrich Berlin (1988:25-28), it is apparent that the children of colaní often became colaní themselves. [...] However, according to Berlin (1988:25), anyone acquiring a book, finding a colaní to take them as an apprentice, and willing to study could become a colaní." [269]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The colaní used their ritual books to serve the needs of commoners, although it is possible that nobles consulted them as well." [270]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "The colaní used their ritual books to serve the needs of commoners, although it is possible that nobles consulted them as well." [271] Thomas Cressy: The quote seems to infer that everyone could participate in this, so I have coded this as 'not applicable' as it was blank.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ patron deity ritual ♥ NOTE: The following refers to the seventeenth century, but it seems like a fairly reasonable inference that the ritual system described by Spanish witnesses at that time emerged in the Post-Classic phase. "A major ceremony in honor of the patron deity of the city-state, usually Pezeelao, was held on a specific day assigned to him in the sacred calendar, perhaps the last day of the ritual cycle, 13 Lao or Pece Lao, his probable namesake. Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart. This ceremony involved a human sacrifice, usually a war captive, and was accompanied by feasting, drinking, music, and dancing, as were all major ceremonies. [...] David Tavarez (2005) has reported ritual songs in early eighteenth-century documents from the Sierra Juarez that relate to Zapotec deities. It seems likely that these ritual songs were sung at one or another of these ceremonies honoring each of these deities. In Zapotec, the songs were called dij dola nicachi, or 'wooden drum songs,' and were performed in a plaza in front of the entire community by musicians (belao in Zapotec) including singers who sang the songs to the beat of wooden drums (nicachi in Zapotec, teponaxtli in Nahuatl)." [303] "The ceremonies [in honour of the patron deity] involved the participation of all the communities within the city-state whose member brought offerings of quail, turkeys, dogs, feathers, and precious stones to the major temple in the capital. The high point of the celebration involved the sacrifice of a war captive who was placed over a sacrificial stone in the temple and had his heart removed and offered to the patron deity." [304]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart." [305] Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart." [306] Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "Because this day was in the sacred calendar of 260 days, it was a moveable ceremony with respect to the solar year and could even occur twice within a given solar year, although 260 days apart." [307] Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. Thomas Cressy: Coded as not applicable due to the quote for inclusion stating all communities were participants
♠ Duration ♣ [12-18] ♥ hours. 1 day. "A major ceremony in honor of the patron deity of the city-state, usually Pezeelao, was held on a specific day assigned to him in the sacred calendar, perhaps the last day of the ritual cycle, 13 Lao or Pece Lao, his probable namesake." Being second sons of nobles, they were aligned with the political elite of these city-states." [309] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The ceremonies [in honour of the patron deity] involved the participation of all the communities within the city-state whose member brought offerings of quail, turkeys, dogs, feathers, and precious stones to the major temple in the capital." Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ annual confession ♥ NOTE: The following refers to the seventeenth century, but it seems like a fairly reasonable inference that the ritual system described by Spanish witnesses at that time emerged in the Post-Classic phase. "Finally, Burgoa (1989: II, 229-31) reports a ceremony carried out near San Francisco Caxonos in AD 1652 that would have been conducted in front of a temple in Prehispanic times. [...]
"A large group of townspeople gathered in front of the deity and the old priest. All the people sat in a fetal position with their heads hung down and cried. One by one they came forth with a small cloth bundle from which they removed corn husks tied at the center. The tied corn husks represented the sins they had committed during the previous year. They placed the husks on the circular mat before the deity to forgive their sins. Then they drew blood from their ears or tongue and captured it on feathers of different colors that they placed on the circular mat as an offering to the deity." [343] "This ceremony is not reported to have been accompanied by feasting, drinking, music, and dancing" [344].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "The tied corn husks represented the sins they had committed during the previous year." [345] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "The tied corn husks represented the sins they had committed during the previous year." [346] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "The tied corn husks represented the sins they had committed during the previous year." [347] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources. Thomas Cressy: Changed code from 'yearly' to 'not applicable'. The ritual description seems to create a picture of townspeople as participants, not spectators. So I have coded this as 'not applicable'.
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "Temple priests staffed the temples (yoho pehe) of the capital and communities within the city-states. Being second sons of nobles, they were aligned with the political elite of these city-states." [348] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ Spanish sources do not indicate that these rituals were in any way exclusive [349]. Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Spanish sources do not indicate that these rituals were in any way exclusive [350]. ♠ fear ♣ inferred present ♥ Because this ritual centred on a confession of one's sins to the gods [353], it seems reasonable to infer that participants would have been afraid that the gods would not have forgiven them. Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.

♠ pain ♣ inferred present ♥ "Then they drew blood from their ears or tongue and captured it on feathers of different colors that they placed on the circular mat as an offering to the deity." [357] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.
♠ mutilation ♣ inferred absent ♥ The main dysphoric act in this ritual appears to have been autosacrificial bloodletting [358]. Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.

♠ other ♣ inferred present ♥ Contrition. "All the people sat in a fetal position with their heads hung down and cried." [360] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources.

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC From Phase 1: The process of political balkanisation which started at the end of the MA IIIA period continued into this period until the Spanish invasion. Separate kingdoms formed, with Monte Albán still occupied but with a much reduced population.[383] "The data suggest that the Early Postclassic saw a significant reduction in inequality and it may have taken two or three centuries for strong social hierarchies to reemerge. The period of relative equality ends between ad 1000 and 1200 with the founding of new ruling dynasties." [384] "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. LATE POSTCLASSIC From Ritual: Rulers were legitimated by their descent from divine ancestors: “The significance of the Mitla murals therefore lies in the nature of their narrative content and their particular placement within the architectural setting. The Tolteca-Chichimeca, Mixtec, and Zapotec lords of Oaxaca who met in these courts all claimed a form of divine status through descent from the three groups of ancestors portrayed in the murals. These ancestors were miraculously created from environmental phenomena: caves, trees, and the heavens. Ancestor worship therefore became not only a primary religious concern but also a means of determining class rank, paramountcy, and titles to elite domains.” [385] From Phase 1: Descriptions in the Spanish relaciones (Spanish written documents from the time of the Spanish conquest) provide evidence for the presence of military officers in charge of competing armies at the end of this period. Their presence in the centuries before the Spanish conquest is inferred.[386][387]

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC:

From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. "Liobaa-phase offerings recovered in TPAs, especially those in the North and South Platforms, consisted of thousands of objects resulting from repeated rituals over the course of several centuries, which suggests the continuous “feeding” of sacrificial offerings to the divine (Herrera 2002; A. Joyce 2009a). The use of common objects such as miniature utilitarian ceramics and obsidian artifacts suggests that a much broader range of people participated in these rituals relative to the highly restricted practices of the Classic period. Offerings in TPAs commemorated earlier ceremonies and the deeper history of the Main Plaza as a sacred mountain of creation. Yet the rituals also created new memories of a sacred past and a cosmic creation that was no longer dominated by powerful nobles, but instead was the product of the sacred work of a much wider range of people, thereby constituting the more egalitarian social relations of the Early Postclassic. Early Postclassic offerings at Monte Albán are related to an expansion of similar rituals in other ruined sites and caves, involving communication with and sacrifice to gods and ancestors in places viewed as interfaces with the divine (Winter et al. 2007:195- 6)." [422] LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: Spanish written records describe the presence of full-time priests (bigaña) who lived in temples and were the sons of the Zapotec nobility. A profesional priesthood is therefore inferred to be present, although the extent to which this information can be extended back to the entire period is not known.[423][424] From Ritual: Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "Temple priests staffed the temples (yoho pehe) of the capital and communities within the city-states. Being second sons of nobles, they were aligned with the political elite of these city-states." [425] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources. From Religion: “The codical appearances of oracles not only as great priests, but also as arbitrators in dynastic disputes between Lord 8 Deer, Lady 6 Monkey, and Lord 4 Wind are confirmed in Burgoa’s references to the powers with which Oaxacan oracles were invested. For instance, the oracle of Achiutla was said to have had superior authority over the Mixtec kings in deciding matters of peace and war. Burgoa (1934a 25: 319; Pohl 1994) even refers to temple armies that the priests could use to enforce their decisions. The ‘’Vuijatao’’ at Mitla was likewise said to have had superior authority over the Zapotec kings in many affairs of state (Burgoa 1934a 26: 125). [...] In conclusion, the oracles of Oaxaca were not rulers in their own right, but rather they were empowered to ajudicate those aspects of the social order that entailed problems of divine inheritance and the well being of extended royal kinship structures as a whole. This view is consistent with Spanish references to the oracles not as kings, but as late renaissance archbishops, or as in the case of Mitla, a ‘pope’.” [426]

The ‘’Vuijatao’’ at Mitla was likewise said to have had superior authority over the Zapotec kings in many affairs of state (Burgoa 1934a 26: 125). [...] In conclusion, the oracles of Oaxaca were not rulers in their own right, but rather they were empowered to ajudicate those aspects of the social order that entailed problems of divine inheritance and the well being of extended royal kinship structures as a whole. This view is consistent with Spanish references to the oracles not as kings, but as late renaissance archbishops, or as in the case of Mitla, a ‘pope’.” [436]

♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC:

From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. "Liobaa-phase offerings recovered in TPAs, especially those in the North and South Platforms, consisted of thousands of objects resulting from repeated rituals over the course of several centuries, which suggests the continuous “feeding” of sacrificial offerings to the divine (Herrera 2002; A. Joyce 2009a). The use of common objects such as miniature utilitarian ceramics and obsidian artifacts suggests that a much broader range of people participated in these rituals relative to the highly restricted practices of the Classic period. Offerings in TPAs commemorated earlier ceremonies and the deeper history of the Main Plaza as a sacred mountain of creation. Yet the rituals also created new memories of a sacred past and a cosmic creation that was no longer dominated by powerful nobles, but instead was the product of the sacred work of a much wider range of people, thereby constituting the more egalitarian social relations of the Early Postclassic. Early Postclassic offerings at Monte Albán are related to an expansion of similar rituals in other ruined sites and caves, involving communication with and sacrifice to gods and ancestors in places viewed as interfaces with the divine (Winter et al. 2007:195- 6)." [437] LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: Spanish written records describe the presence of full-time priests (bigaña) who lived in temples and were the sons of the Zapotec nobility. A profesional priesthood is therefore inferred to be present, although the extent to which this information can be extended back to the entire period is not known.[438][439] From Ritual: Inferred from seventeenth-century ethnohistoric sources. "Temple priests staffed the temples (yoho pehe) of the capital and communities within the city-states. Being second sons of nobles, they were aligned with the political elite of these city-states." [440] Inferred from seventeenth-century rituals as described by Spanish sources. From Religion: “The codical appearances of oracles not only as great priests, but also as arbitrators in dynastic disputes between Lord 8 Deer, Lady 6 Monkey, and Lord 4 Wind are confirmed in Burgoa’s references to the powers with which Oaxacan oracles were invested. For instance, the oracle of Achiutla was said to have had superior authority over the Mixtec kings in deciding matters of peace and war. Burgoa (1934a 25: 319; Pohl 1994) even refers to temple armies that the priests could use to enforce their decisions. The ‘’Vuijatao’’ at Mitla was likewise said to have had superior authority over the Zapotec kings in many affairs of state (Burgoa 1934a 26: 125). [...] In conclusion, the oracles of Oaxaca were not rulers in their own right, but rather they were empowered to ajudicate those aspects of the social order that entailed problems of divine inheritance and the well being of extended royal kinship structures as a whole. This view is consistent with Spanish references to the oracles not as kings, but as late renaissance archbishops, or as in the case of Mitla, a ‘pope’.” [441]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: The process of political balkanisation which started at the end of the MA IIIA period continued into this period until the Spanish invasion. Separate kingdoms formed, with Monte Albán still occupied but with a much reduced population.[457] "The data suggest that the Early Postclassic saw a significant reduction in inequality and it may have taken two or three centuries for strong social hierarchies to reemerge. The period of relative equality ends between ad 1000 and 1200 with the founding of new ruling dynasties." [458] From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[459] The levels of organisation are supported by the archaeological evidence which suggests that there was a head town in each of the polities, with supporting villages and hamlets.[460] There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[461]

♠ Land ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse..."The data suggest that the Early Postclassic saw a significant reduction in inequality and it may have taken two or three centuries for strong social hierarchies to reemerge. The period of relative equality ends between ad 1000 and 1200 with the founding of new ruling dynasties." [517] LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[518]Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[519] The levels of organisation are supported by the archaeological evidence which suggests that there was a head town in each of the polities, with supporting villages and hamlets.[520] There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[521]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse..."The data suggest that the Early Postclassic saw a significant reduction in inequality and it may have taken two or three centuries for strong social hierarchies to reemerge. The period of relative equality ends between ad 1000 and 1200 with the founding of new ruling dynasties." [637] LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Ritual: Rulers were legitimated by their descent from divine ancestors: “The significance of the Mitla murals therefore lies in the nature of their narrative content and their particular placement within the architectural setting. The Tolteca-Chichimeca, Mixtec, and Zapotec lords of Oaxaca who met in these courts all claimed a form of divine status through descent from the three groups of ancestors portrayed in the murals. These ancestors were miraculously created from environmental phenomena: caves, trees, and the heavens. Ancestor worship therefore became not only a primary religious concern but also a means of determining class rank, paramountcy, and titles to elite domains.” [638] “Unlike the Tolteca-Chichimeca, who preferred to cremate their dead, Mixtecs and Zapotecs mummified the dead at sacred shrines such as Chalcatongo and Mitla. Mummy bundles served as physical proof of the ranking lines of succession upon which both Mixtec and Zapotec social order was defined. The maintenance of a funerary cult that focused on the grouping of the remains of the royal dead in central places was ingenious. In societies like those of the Mixtec and Zapotec, rank was traced through one’s familial relationship with the oldest royal lineages. By controlling access to the divine dead and managing their cult, oracles were actually manipulating the affairs of the living descendants, as kings and queens were forced to use them as mediators in the lineage disputes. Funerary cults therefore provided a source of corporate identity, ensuring political stability among various members of the competitive royal kin groups in the absence of any overarching capital in Postclassic Oaxaca (Pohl 1994c: 69-82, 1999).” [639]

♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ inferred absent ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. "The sixty-one colaní recorded by Balslobre included five women[...]. Although colaní were predominantly males, the inclusion of women is not surprising in Zapotec culture where, since Prehispanic times, males and females were relatively equal in status (Lind and Urcid 2010:11). From the list of sixty-one colaní cited by Heinrich Berlin (1988:25-28), it is apparent that the children of colaní often became colaní themselves. [...] However, according to Berlin (1988:25), anyone acquiring a book, finding a colaní to take them as an apprentice, and willing to study could become a colaní." Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ present ♥ From Phase 1: Marriage alliances between the elite of the Zapotec and Mixtec societies during this period were recorded by the relaciones (16th century Spanish writers). Alliances were created for many possible reasons, including political gain, status and increased access to farmland and resources.[655] No permanent unions between polities; these alliances seem between individuals. From Religion: “Marriage among commoners probably was mostly monogamous, although polygyny was permitted if one could support more than one wife.” [656] “’’Caciques’’, as did the rest of the nobility, generally practiced polygyny, as specifically indicated in the ‘’relaciones’’ of Teocuicuiloc, Huitzo, and Tehuantepec and in other sources. In huitzo, for example, ‘’caciques’’ married as many as fifteen or twenty women in pre-Spanish times.” [657] From Religion: “Both men and women were accorded rank in Zapotec society, at least in the noble estate.” [658] However, in commoner marriages, the man was allowed to take the woman by force if she did not consent to their union, as long as her family approved [659]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ From Phase 1: Marriage alliances between the elite of the Zapotec and Mixtec societies during this period were recorded by the relaciones (16th century Spanish writers). Alliances were created for many possible reasons, including political gain, status and increased access to farmland and resources.[660] No permanent unions between polities; these alliances seem between individuals. From Phase 1: "The scale of the palaces at Yagul and Mitla suggests an increase in the size of the ruling family perhaps resulting from polygamous marriages. The number of co-resident support personnel of ruling families, including retainers and slaves, may have increased as well. A probable commoner residence excavated at Macuilxóchitl with two interconnected patio groups (Winter et al. 2007:200- 2) indicates that the size of some non-elite households also may have been greater than during earlier periods. From Religion: “Both men and women were accorded rank in Zapotec society, at least in the noble estate.” [661] However, in commoner marriages, the man was allowed to take the woman by force if she did not consent to their union, as long as her family approved [662]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: "The scale of the palaces at Yagul and Mitla suggests an increase in the size of the ruling family perhaps resulting from polygamous marriages. The number of co-resident support personnel of ruling families, including retainers and slaves, may have increased as well. A probable commoner residence excavated at Macuilxóchitl with two interconnected patio groups (Winter et al. 2007:200- 2) indicates that the size of some non-elite households also may have been greater than during earlier periods. From Religion: “Both men and women were accorded rank in Zapotec society, at least in the noble estate.” [663] However, in commoner marriages, the man was allowed to take the woman by force if she did not consent to their union, as long as her family approved [664]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Social class ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[669] From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. ♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[672] From Religion: Inferred from the fact that, at Mitla, the pre-eminent ritual and religious centre of the Oaxacan Postclassic, murals portray the cosmogonies of all three of the major ethnic groups that ruled over the region at the time: “The portrayal of the three Oaxacan cosmogonies further demonstrates that the site did not subscribe to any one of the several competing alliance corridors that defined Tolteca-Chichimeca, Mixtec, and Zapotec factionalism, but rather the oracle’s palaces and courts represented a sacred space in which all were united and recognized as equals.” [673] Indeed, Oudijk writes: “I have come to the conclusion that ethnicity was not an important factor in Postclassic Oaxaca but rather factionalism and interfactional relationships were responsible for shaping Postclassic Oaxaca.” [674]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Religion: “Both men and women were accorded rank in Zapotec society, at least in the noble estate.” [675] However, in commoner marriages, the man was allowed to take the woman by force if she did not consent to their union, as long as her family approved [676] Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[677] From Phase 1: Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[678] The levels of organisation are supported by the archaeological evidence which suggests that there was a head town in each of the polities, with supporting villages and hamlets.[679]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1: "The most dramatic changes involved the collapse of ruling institutions and dynasties. The evidence demonstrates that ruling institutions failed and nobles lost the bulk of their wealth and political authority. Temples, elaborate residences, and ceremonial spaces previously controlled by the nobility were abandoned. Monumental art and writing ceased. The evidence at present suggests a profound collapse in ruling institutions as well as in settlement and perhaps demography, to the extent that it might be viewed as a rupture in the dominant discourse. Additional research is crucial to better understand this critical period in Oaxaca’s prehispanic history, however. Additional chronological refinements are needed along the lines of Markens’ recent work (Markens 2004, 2008; Martínez López et al. 2000), as well as reanalyses of survey data and excavations in smaller communities that may not have undergone major demographic disruptions. LATE POSTCLASSIC: Evidence for bureaucracy during this period is mainly limited to the documents written after the Spanish invasion.[700] From Phase 1: Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[701] The levels of organisation are supported by the archaeological evidence which suggests that there was a head town in each of the polities, with supporting villages and hamlets.[702] From Religion: Inferred from the fact that, at Mitla, the pre-eminent ritual and religious centre of the Oaxacan Postclassic, murals portray the cosmogonies of all three of the major ethnic groups that ruled over the region at the time: “The portrayal of the three Oaxacan cosmogonies further demonstrates that the site did not subscribe to any one of the several competing alliance corridors that defined Tolteca-Chichimeca, Mixtec, and Zapotec factionalism, but rather the oracle’s palaces and courts represented a sacred space in which all were united and recognized as equals.” [703] Indeed, Oudijk writes: “I have come to the conclusion that ethnicity was not an important factor in Postclassic Oaxaca but rather factionalism and interfactional relationships were responsible for shaping Postclassic Oaxaca.” [704]

♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ As there are no relevant written texts or supporting archaeological evidence there is no indication of the type of property rights employed at this time. GJ

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ EARLY POSTCLASSIC: From Phase 1:"The data suggest that the Early Postclassic saw a significant reduction in inequality and it may have taken two or three centuries for strong social hierarchies to reemerge. The period of relative equality ends between ad 1000 and 1200 with the founding of new ruling dynasties." [705] LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Ritual: Rulers were legitimated by their descent from divine ancestors: “The significance of the Mitla murals therefore lies in the nature of their narrative content and their particular placement within the architectural setting. The Tolteca-Chichimeca, Mixtec, and Zapotec lords of Oaxaca who met in these courts all claimed a form of divine status through descent from the three groups of ancestors portrayed in the murals. These ancestors were miraculously created from environmental phenomena: caves, trees, and the heavens. Ancestor worship therefore became not only a primary religious concern but also a means of determining class rank, paramountcy, and titles to elite domains.” [706] “Unlike the Tolteca-Chichimeca, who preferred to cremate their dead, Mixtecs and Zapotecs mummified the dead at sacred shrines such as Chalcatongo and Mitla. Mummy bundles served as physical proof of the ranking lines of succession upon which both Mixtec and Zapotec social order was defined. The maintenance of a funerary cult that focused on the grouping of the remains of the royal dead in central places was ingenious. In societies like those of the Mixtec and Zapotec, rank was traced through one’s familial relationship with the oldest royal lineages. By controlling access to the divine dead and managing their cult, oracles were actually manipulating the affairs of the living descendants, as kings and queens were forced to use them as mediators in the lineage disputes. Funerary cults therefore provided a source of corporate identity, ensuring political stability among various members of the competitive royal kin groups in the absence of any overarching capital in Postclassic Oaxaca (Pohl 1994c: 69-82, 1999).” [707] From Religion: “The division into commoners and nobles was accompanied by sumptuary rules pertaining to kinds of dress and ornamentation, diet, and linguistic habit. The ‘’caciques’’ or Huitzo, for example, dressed in brightly woven cotton mantles and loincloths while the common people wore clothing made of maguey fiber. Also, at Huitzo only ‘’caciques’’ and ‘’principales’’ were allowed to eat turkey, deer, hares, and rabbits. [...] Commoners employed a special set of pronouns when addressing a noble. A special type of verb, which Cordova called reverential, also was employed only by commoners speaking with nobles.” [708]

From the list of sixty-one colaní cited by Heinrich Berlin (1988:25-28), it is apparent that the children of colaní often became colaní themselves. [...] However, according to Berlin (1988:25), anyone acquiring a book, finding a colaní to take them as an apprentice, and willing to study could become a colaní." [716]

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ suspected unknown ♥ LATE POSTCLASSIC: From Religion: “Slaves were obtained in war, could be bought or sold, were sacrificed in ceremonies, and were used as domestic servants. A number of ‘’relaciones’’ attest that slaves were taken in war and used in ceremonies involving human sacrifice; this fact is reported in Teitipac, Huitzo, Ixtepeji, Miahuatlan, and Tehuantepec. The Cordova dictionary gives the following entry: ‘Man whom they sacrifice as a result of being taken in war or a captive presented to their lord in order to be sacrificed. ‘’Peniyypeniquij’’, ‘’peniye’’, ‘’xoyaa’’.’//Slaves were bought and sold; some were used for servants.” [726]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Normative Ideology

"At Monte Albán, Liobaa-phase evidence shows that as the architecture of the Main Plaza fell to ruins people returned to conduct rituals in the empty ceremonial center (Herrera 2002; A. Joyce 2009a). Ritual practices involved the placement of offerings in Classic-period tombs and TPAs. The most common objects in ritual deposits were miniature ceramic vessels, incense burners, penates (small stone figurines), and obsidian blades, all of which were associated with death, sacrifice, and ancestors. The data indicate that rituals in TPAs and tombs were being carried out to contact deities and ancestors to petition them for health and prosperity and perhaps to bring harm to enemies.

"Zapotecs removed the contents of several tombs from Classic-period highstatus residences and replaced them with offerings of miniature vessels and penates. The people who reused tombs must have known their location and may have been the descendants of the nobles who had lived in the residences and were buried in the tombs. The removal of bones from ancestral tombs referenced memories of a glorious past, but also the changed circumstances of Early Postclassic nobles now removed from their spatial and symbolic association with the sacred mountain and apparently living in much humbler circumstances in communities on the valley floor.

"Liobaa-phase offerings recovered in TPAs, especially those in the North and South Platforms, consisted of thousands of objects resulting from repeated rituals over the course of several centuries, which suggests the continuous “feeding” of sacrificial offerings to the divine (Herrera 2002; A. Joyce 2009a). The use of common objects such as miniature utilitarian ceramics and obsidian artifacts suggests that a much broader range of people participated in these rituals relative to the highly restricted practices of the Classic period. Offerings in TPAs commemorated earlier ceremonies and the deeper history of the Main Plaza as a sacred mountain of creation. Yet the rituals also created new memories of a sacred past and a cosmic creation that was no longer dominated by powerful nobles, but instead was the product of the sacred work of a much wider range of people, thereby constituting the more egalitarian social relations of the Early Postclassic. Early Postclassic offerings at Monte Albán are related to an expansion of similar rituals in other ruined sites and caves, involving communication with and sacrifice to gods and ancestors in places viewed as interfaces with the divine (Winter et al. 2007:195- 6)." [736]


Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. For example, rulers are blessed by gods; the institution of kingship is ordained by heaven

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Do rulers control supernatural energy or is the energy entirely in the possession of the god/s. For example, mana.

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown

♠ Automatic deification ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Rulers become gods after their death as a matter of course (for instance, Egyptian pharaohs), as opposed to rulers who are posthumously deified as a special privilege, even if this privilege becomes a de facto guarantee (for instance, Roman Emperors)

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown.

People from certain areas (i.e. holy centers, such as Israel or Mecca, are seen as superior or have special privileges (tax breaks, etc.) compared to people from other places, regardless of whether other equalities are also observed, for instance gender

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Religious doctrine, philosophical statements, or practice makes claims about inequality. For instance, explicit statements by religious groups that a certain gender or social group is inherently superior to others

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Religious doctrine, philosophical statements, or practice encourages or demands monogamy among its adherents

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Religious doctrine, philosophical statements, or practice makes claims about engaging in activity for the benefit of a wider community, for instance Christian traditions of alms-giving or Islamic sadaqah

♠ charity ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown
♠ production of public goods ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Public Goods refer to anything that incurs cost to an individual or group of individuals, but that can be used or enjoyed by others who did not incur any of the cost, namely the public at large. They are non-excludable and non-rivalrous goods. Examples are roads, public drinking fountains, public parks or theatres, temples open to the public, etc.
♠ communal dining ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Religious doctrine, philosophical statements, or practice encourages or demands that members of the religious system dine communally, for instance breaking of Ramadan fasting in the Islamic tradition
♠ morality ♣ ♥ absent/present/unknown. Any claims made by religious doctrine, philosophical statements, or practice about proper moral behavior. Include anything that is not covered below

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

The following provides information on ritual and religion in the region at the time of Spanish contact. It is unclear how long these beliefs and practices had existed by the time they were observed, so they may not be relevant to this specific polity. Expert input needed.

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code "compound" moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).

Human Enforcement of Morality
